  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_225169109" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/225169109" />
    <a title="Sandy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6043535-sandy"><img alt="Sandy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1314365393p2/6043535.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/225169109?book_show_action=true">Oct 19, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sandy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sandy" href="/user/show/6043535-sandy">Sandy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer225169109" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15301814023057885384">Tim Powers' fourth novel, 1983's "The Anubis Gates," is a book that I had been meaning to read for years. Chosen for inclusion in both David Pringle's "Modern Fantasy: The Hundred Best Novels" and Jones & Newman's "Horror: 100 Best Books," as well as the recipient of the Philip K. Dick Memorial Award in 1984, the book came with plenty of good word of mouth, to say the least. And, as it turns out, all the ballyhoo back when was fully justified, as this really IS some kind of superb work. As John </span>
  <span id="freeText15301814023057885384" style="display:none">Tim Powers' fourth novel, 1983's "The Anubis Gates," is a book that I had been meaning to read for years. Chosen for inclusion in both David Pringle's "Modern Fantasy: The Hundred Best Novels" and Jones &amp; Newman's "Horror: 100 Best Books," as well as the recipient of the Philip K. Dick Memorial Award in 1984, the book came with plenty of good word of mouth, to say the least. And, as it turns out, all the ballyhoo back when was fully justified, as this really IS some kind of superb work. As John Clute puts it in the Jones &amp; Newman volume, it is "a book which it is possible (rare praise) to love"; as Pringle writes, it is a "virtuoso performance." I could not agree more.<br /><br />In the novel, we meet a middle-aged widower named Brendan Doyle, an expert on Samuel Taylor Coleridge and the (fictional) poet William Ashbless. Doyle is asked by an eccentric millionaire who has come up with a time travel device to journey back to London in the year 1810, to attend a Coleridge lecture with a group of wealthy chrono tourists. Doyle warily agrees and--to make things brief--gets marooned in the past, where he soon becomes enmeshed in the machinations of Egyptian wizards attempting to destroy England. Powers' way-out plot somehow manages to conflate the brainwashed "ka" of Lord Byron, a body-hopping werewolf, an underground criminal society headed by a deformed clown on stilts, a plucky young vengeance-seeking woman disguised as a man, Egyptian gods, 4" high "Spoonsize Boys," fire and wind elementals, the Mameluke slaughter of 1811, a menagerie of freaks, the Beatles (!) and on and on. And just when the reader thinks this plot could not possibly get any wilder, Powers catapults Doyle back even further, to the year 1684! Indeed, there is no way for anyone to possibly guess what is coming next, in this truly zany romp of a book. Remarkably, every single page of this nearly 400-page affair boasts some startling conversation, plot twist, description or speculation. Powers has done a huge amount of historical research, and his book always has the ring of verisimilitude, despite the outrageousness of the plot. An originator not only of the so-called "steampunk" literary genre but also of the "secret histories" style of writing, Powers, in this book, puts forth his amusing explanations for London's Great Fire of 1666, as well as Byron's apparently simultaneous presences in Greece and London in the autumn of 1810. And although stories with time travel paradoxes can sometimes leave me with a headache, I found this one absolutely delightful. Let me not mince words: This book is a blast, from its opening scene in a London gypsy camp in 1802 to its wonderful, ironic, totally satisfying conclusion in the swamps of Woolwich. Clute was right; I really DO love this book, and indeed am in awe of it. So many wild elements mixed together, such an original and imaginative story line, and the whole thing coming together so completely and perfectly...Tim Powers must be some kind of a freakin' genius! I'm gonna need more of this guy; possibly his 1979 novel "The Drawing of the Dark," which is highly praised in Cawthorn &amp; Moorcock's "Fantasy: The 100 Best Books"....<br /><br />I would like to add that "The Anubis Gates" is not an easy read for folks who (like me) choose to look up every historical reference or place name that they encounter. I found a London street map invaluable while reading this book, for example; it's not necessary, of course, but sure does make for a richer, deeper experience. Thus, I was able to spot one of two flubs that Powers is guilty of in his otherwise perfect work. At one point, he tells us that Coleman Street is east of Bishopsgate Street, whereas a quick look at the map will clearly show that it is west. Powers' other goof? When he infers on page 353 that Doyle would be attending a literary meeting at the home of renowned publisher John Murray on a Tuesday, and two pages later says it would be on a Monday. (I also find it hard to believe that the word "savvy" was being used in 1684.) Mere quibbles, of course. For all lovers of sci-fi, fantasy, horror, historical fiction and/or poetry, this book should prove a godsend. It is a very generous book, far more intelligent and humorous than it absolutely needs to be, and well deserving of all the accolades that it has received. Oh...and I just love the inclusion of that pig Latin! </span>
  <a data-text-id="15301814023057885384" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

It is also true, that I am very conservative, also in the realm of fantasy, and may not be ready for a style such as the Steampunk Tim Powers practice. Reminded me of that wonderful movie of Barry Levinson The Young Sherlock Holmes, where a group of worshippers of the ancient gods of Egypt are engaged in abducting damsels, and sacrifice them, amen to kill a group of archaeologists who were there. Also I liked the contribution's to the subject of alternative universes, and even a skeptic. To Cesar what is Cesar <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/261499.Time_Patrol?ac=1&amp;from_search=true">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2...</a> <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/32278.Poul_Anderson">https://www.goodreads.com/author/show...</a> with all this novel if I find it slightly higher than "Timeline" of Michael Crichton <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7669.Timeline?ac=1&amp;from_search=true">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7...</a> is certainly very interesting theory of Tim Powers of holes in time, based on the teachings of the pre-Socratic philosophers in this case Heraclitus, and comparing the time with a river. It almost made me remember the book by Ben Aaronovitch "Rivers of London" <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9317452-rivers-of-london">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9...</a>. There is a real England, and another magic in which governs the Raven King. Between the Kaa, the machinations of the evil millionaire with cancer, and Amenofis Fikke, which shifts body rather than Dragon Ball of am (had to show my Otaku side) It is a real mess with so much change of body, I did not know who was who. Or where were the characters. Some scenes will until found me unpleasant. Not to mention the Egyptian part, I think, which is the worst novel. In fact, many of the subplots come not to tell, and unnecessarily lengthen this novel Therefore initially had suspended her, because he thought that Powers in her next novel (because this will not be the last book, which read that insurance) would do it much better. That yes the musical taste of the unfortunate protagonist. It is that to sing the Beatles. The 1960s are not my favorites only demons they like, they ask it to Azazel in the film fail. That, I hope, whoever reads this criticism like you more than me. That, it will return to meet Mr Powers in the future, or the past:-).<br />............................................................................................................................................................................<br />Damas y caballeros este fue el último libro, que leí antes de irme de vacaciones y hacer un parón forzoso en Semana Santa (que se juntó con el día de Villalar). La historia de este libro estuvo implicada la mano de una gran dama a quien le prometí leer “Las puertas de Anubis” después con mi mala experiencia con “Hacia costas extrañas” uno de los libros que más me ha desagradado leer. A esto se mezcló una cosa, y es que jamás me han gustado los piratas. En el carácter hispano está siempre el odio al pirata al menos en mi caso. <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15670.On_Stranger_Tides?ac=1&amp;from_search=true">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1...</a> Tenía la extraña sensación de que la edición que leí de Roca estaba mal traducida, y se juntó con que hubo partes de la novela en que no me enteré de nada, y encima era más rara que un perro verde. Con todo Tim Powers es un escritor que desde el plano humano siempre me ha caído bien, y siempre le he tenido simpatía, incluso cuando era niño e iba a la librería Petrarca cuando estaba en La Salle (ahí me compre los libros de Héroes de la Dragonlance), pero nunca me atreví a comprar sus libros. También era un escritor que recibía elogios de autores a los que estimo como mi adorado Joseph Pearce <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/11627.Joseph_Pearce">https://www.goodreads.com/author/show...</a> que llegó a calificar a “Last Call” <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/209690.Last_Call?ac=1&amp;from_search=true">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2...</a> como una de sus novelas favoritas, y si lo dice el gran Joseph Pearce es tan verdadero como los dogmas de la Iglesia Católica. También ha hablado maravillas de él Sandra Miesel <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/61544.Sandra_Miesel">https://www.goodreads.com/author/show...</a> y además dió una entrevista muy chula a Ignatius Insight <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.ignatiusinsight.com/features2005/tpowers_intvw_sept05.asp">www.ignatiusinsight.com/features2005/...</a> además luego descubrí que fue amigo de Philip K. Dick, y estuvo con él hasta el final <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.religionenlibertad.com/cultura/11161/el-psicodelico-catolicismo-de-philip-k-dick-el-padre-de-blade.html">https://www.religionenlibertad.com/cu...</a> (aunque debo confesar, que tampoco Philip K. Dick me gusta mucho, salvo en el cine <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4764.Philip_K_Dick">https://www.goodreads.com/author/show...</a> . En resumen con Tim Powers me pasa lo mismo que a J.R.R. Tolkien <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/656983.J_R_R_Tolkien">https://www.goodreads.com/author/show...</a> con las Crónicas de Narnia <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11127.The_Chronicles_of_Narnia?ac=1&amp;from_search=true">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1...</a> que por mucho que me guste el creador de sus libros no consiguen gustarme sus libros. Como ya dije en mi crítica de “Space Chantey” de R.A. Lafferty <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2701399528?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a> <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4471254.R_A_Lafferty">https://www.goodreads.com/author/show...</a> hay escritores a los que amas, y a los que odias. Mi amigo Kunnio lo llamaría filia, y fobias instintivas. He conseguido que me guste R.A. Lafferty (que muchos dicen que es raro), Fred Saberhagen <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/10082.Fred_Saberhagen">https://www.goodreads.com/author/show...</a> (que me encanta. Comparto esa pasión con el escritor Charles E. Yallowitz <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6965804.Charles_E_Yallowitz">https://www.goodreads.com/author/show...</a> ), también me he enganchado al estilo de Dean R. Koontz <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6926240.Dean_R_Koontz">https://www.goodreads.com/author/show...</a> (aunque sus novelas sean muy parecidas). Pero igual que los pobres Basil de “El cáliz de plata” <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/122771.The_Silver_Chalice?ac=1&amp;from_search=true">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1...</a> y Kitty Fane (el primero no podía esculpir el rostro de Cristo, porque le faltaba el don de la gracia, y la segunda quería amar a su marido, pero no podía en “El velo pintado” <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/99664.The_Painted_Veil?ac=1&amp;from_search=true">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9...</a> ) No consigo que me guste del todo. También es verdad, que yo soy muy conservador, también en el terreno de la fantasía, y quizás no esté preparado para un estilo como es el Steampunk que practica Tim Powers. Con todo, creo, que con “Las puertas de Anubis” ha obtenido éxitos relativos. Me agrado mucho el tema de una orden secreta egipcia, que trata de destruir Inglaterra. Me recordó a esa maravillosa película de Barry Levinson The Young Sherlock Holmes, dónde un grupo de adoradores de los antiguos dioses de Egipto se dedican a secuestrar doncellas, y sacrificarlas, amén de matar a un grupo de arqueólogos que estuvieron allí. También me ha gustado mucho la aportación de al tema de los universos alternativos, y aunque sea un escéptico. Yo creo en la paradoja de Fermi soy muy escéptico en lo que respecta a los viajes en el tiempo, pero me encanta leer historias sobre ellos. De todas formas, el autor del prólogo de la obra “Las puertas de Anubis” que ha editado Gigamesh le ha hecho un flaco favor. Vale que hay que vender y promover la obra del autor que editas, pero decir que esta es la mejor novela de viajes en el tiempo, y decir que la historia cobra vida a diferencia de algunas novelas históricas, que son puros decorados. A parte de falso es una sandez. Primero, porque hay un autor que ha escrito mejores novelas de viajes en el tiempo que Tim Powers, y se llama Poul Anderson, y ya te digo yo, que como historiador este si puede venderse como un escritor de novela histórica. Creo, que lo habría sido de haberlo querido él. Pero Time Patrol es la mejor novela de viajes en el tiempo, que se ha escrito. Al menos para mi gusto. Al Cesar lo que es del Cesar <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/261499.Time_Patrol?ac=1&amp;from_search=true">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2...</a> <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/32278.Poul_Anderson">https://www.goodreads.com/author/show...</a> con todo esta novela si me parece ligeramente superior a “Timeline” de Michael Crichton <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7669.Timeline?ac=1&amp;from_search=true">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7...</a> desde luego es muy interesante la teoría de Tim Powers de los agujeros en el tiempo, basándose en las enseñanzas de los filósofos presocráticos en este caso Heráclito, y comparando el tiempo con un río. Casi me hizo recordar el libro de Ben Aaronovitch “Ríos de Londres” <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9317452-rivers-of-london">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9...</a> . También me gustaron muchos las digresiones históricas, y que se escogiera a un experto en literatura Brendan Doyle como protagonista, y el papel honroso de Samuel Taylor Coleridge <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/11525.Samuel_Taylor_Coleridge">https://www.goodreads.com/author/show...</a> que siempre me pareció uno de los epígonos más positivos del romanticismo inglés a diferencia de Lord Byron (que también aquí sale) <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/44407.Lord_Byron">https://www.goodreads.com/author/show...</a> y Shelley <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/45882.Percy_Bysshe_Shelley">https://www.goodreads.com/author/show...</a> (este gracias a Dios no sale). También fue un acierto la elección de los villanos el Doctor Romanelli, y hacer otro viaje en el tiempo a la Inglaterra del siglo XVII (por cierto, esa parte es ligeramente católica porque la hermandad egipcia está coaligada con los protestantes. En este caso el traicionero Monmouth hijo natural de Carlos II y los buenos van con los católicos). Los bichos que salían casi me recuerdan a los de la momia (la de Stephen Sommers y Brendan Fraser, no la de Tom Cruise o la más antigua con Boris Karloff). Pero creo, que lo mejor es la recreación del Londres decimonónico, y esos mendigos, que me recordaron a los de nuestra Señora de Paris <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/30597.The_Hunchback_of_Notre_Dame?ac=1&amp;from_search=true">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3...</a> también es otro acierto la presencia del malvado Horrabin. Un payaso tan malvado como el de It <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/830502.It?ac=1&amp;from_search=true">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8...</a> también la historia de amor es muy bonita. La época me recuerda a “Jonathan Strange y el señor Norrell” <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/14201.Jonathan_Strange_Mr_Norrell?ac=1&amp;from_search=true">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1...</a> escrita por Susanah Clarke, y también es historia alternativa en la Inglaterra de la época de las guerras Napoleónicas, dónde dos magos compiten entre si, y se alían llegado el momento para derrotar a la criatura venida de un mundo mágico. Hay una Inglaterra real, y otra mágica en la que gobierna el Rey Cuervo. Pero no diré nada, para no reventar el argumento a nadie, y sobre todo lo mejor de esta novela es el zarpazo al determinismo, y que nuestro futuro no este escrito. Está muy bien haber metido un escritor de ficción como William Ashblee. Pero es que la novela es más rara que un perro verde, y algunas escenas pegaban tanto como a un santo dos pistolas. Demasiado vanguardista para mi gusto. Entre los Kaa, las intrigas del malvado millonario con cáncer, y Amenofis Fikke, que cambia de cuerpo más que Ginew de Dragon Ball (había que mostrar mi lado Otaku). Es un auténtico lio con tanto cambio de cuerpo, yo ya no sabía quién era quien. Ni dónde estaban los personajes. Algunas escenas hasta me parecieron desagradables. Por no hablar de la parte egipcia, que creo, que es lo peor de la novela. De hecho, muchas de las subtramas no vienen a cuento, y alargan esta novela innecesariamente.<br />Por ello en un principio la había suspendido, porque pensaba que Powers en su siguiente novela (porque este no va a ser el último libro, que le lea eso seguro) lo haría mucho mejor. Pero murió Gene Wolfe, al que nunca conseguí entender, y me dije que había que aprobar esta novela, porque es Navidad, como me decían en La Salle (2,5). Eso sí el gusto musical del protagonista lamentable. Que es eso de ponerse a cantar a los Beatles. Los años sesenta no son mis favoritos sólo a los demonios les gustan, sino que se lo pregunten a Azazel en la película Fallen. Eso, sí espero, que quien lea esta crítica le guste más que a mí. Eso, sí volveremos a encontrarnos señor Powers en el futuro, o en el pasado :-). <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="66506504474796894" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer22967072" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3516028378391048240">This was a tough one for me. I’d pick it up, read half a chapter, put it down for a few days, pick it back up --- rinse and repeat. <br/><br/>Obviously, this isn’t my usual reading style. <br/><br/>This took me about 170 pages to really sit back and go ‘whoa’ (Joey Lawrence Style). Once I hit that mark, I was fine. I felt the story was entertaining. Clever, even. I particularly enjoyed the Coleridge and Byron characters. Eventually, I warmed up to the main protagonist, Brendan Doyle, even though I had a hard time t</span>
  <span id="freeText3516028378391048240" style="display:none">This was a tough one for me. I’d pick it up, read half a chapter, put it down for a few days, pick it back up --- rinse and repeat. <br /><br />Obviously, this isn’t my usual reading style. <br /><br />This took me about 170 pages to really sit back and go ‘whoa’ (Joey Lawrence Style). Once I hit that mark, I was fine. I felt the story was entertaining. Clever, even. I particularly enjoyed the Coleridge and Byron characters. Eventually, I warmed up to the main protagonist, Brendan Doyle, even though I had a hard time truly buying into the idea that a 20th century biographer of poets could be such a swash-buckling hero in the 17th and 19th centuries. I felt that there was little there to make me believe he could triumph over such odds. But, that’s just me. <br /><br />I was a bit hazy on the whole Fikee character and the ‘body snatcher’ scenes. It didn’t ring true to me and as I can see this being an important element to the story, I was often bored by it. <br /><br />Overall, I would say 3 ½ stars as I did find it really engaging when it did hook me in. I may read more Tim Powers, but I’m not in a frenzied rush to gobble him up. <br /></span>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('UMB7NDHW+awYBRkv4bklbbEiX1BWqQaJquhI7h6nC8LKiSq5FQhKnLkm2ULFEwhXs8QjGtqmnG/swmVBZZjcxw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

More than perhaps any other Disney attraction, the Haunted Mansion is powered by story: the scenes and spirits you witness from your Doom Buggy all play roles in the overall narrative of the Mansion, and if you don\'t know the story, you\'ll miss out on a lot of the fun, and a few of the scares, too.Baham clues you in about such things as why the Mansion is there, who built it, how it became haunted, and the dastardly deeds done by some of its residents in their corruptible, mortal states.A Taste of What\'s InsideFrom the comfort of your Doom Buggy you\'ll read:\n  How Walt\'s boyhood experience with the haunted Sauer Castle may have inspired Disneyland\'s Haunted Mansion\n  Insider accounts of the creative clashes between Disney Imagineers over whether the Mansion\'s haunts should be humorous or horrific\n  An analysis of the ride, scene by scene, with insight into how the effects work, delightfully eerie trivia, and anecdotes from Mansion Imagineers and Cast Members\n  The stories behind some of the Mansion\'s many denizens, including the Hatbox Ghost, the Knight, the Sea Captain, the Raven, the unhappy couple Constance and George, and the Ghost Host himself\n  Extensive, exclusive commentary by Mansion designer Rolly Crump\n  A summary of the most notable Mansion collectibles released by Disney over the years\nThere\'s always room for one more, and this time you\'re it: come experience the Haunted Mansion with the &quot;lights on&quot; and learn its ghostly history, its sinister secrets, and why this Disney attraction continues to happily haunt fans young and old.Cory Doctorow [BoingBoing.net]: Baham is one of the most trusted, established experts on the Haunted Mansion, a trufan’s trufan.Guillermo del Toro [Award-winning film director, co-creator The Strain]: Having delved into Disney lore for decades and having been immersed in the Haunted Mansion world since age 3, I thought I knew pretty much everything there is about my favorite Disneyland ride ever. Y eso se conoce como \'proceso de investigación\'. Es donde el autor se da cuenta de si una historia va a merecer o no la pena ser contada. Ahí es donde surgen los verdaderos dilemas, los potenciales problemas y innumerables quebraderos de cabeza. Y ahí es, precisamente, donde esta historia inicia su andadura...\n\nMarta Marferny es escritora. Se toma muy a pecho su trabajo y, fiel a sus principios, indaga sin descanso para que sus lectores se sientan cómodos con el trabajo llevado a cabo. Es durante esa indagación cuando, casi de improviso, topa con un antiguo documento. Nada indica que se trate de algo más que un viejo legado, algo que pasará sin pena ni gloria como un simple apoyo para su nueva novela. Sin embargo, Marferny sospecha poco que aquel documento será el principio de una aventura, mayor y más compleja que ninguna de las que su mente haya podido siquiera imaginar. Porque ahora ella se convierte en protagonista de una historia sin final decidido. Aquel viejo documento cambiará no sólo su vida, sino la de miles de personas que pueden verse afectadas por lo que en allí está escrito. \n\nPero, ¿qué misterio se enconde en un viejo documento que puede cambiar el curso de tantas vidas? La respuesta es sencilla. En aquellos legajos se desvelará la existencia de una secta, aparentemente secreta y desconocida que responde al nombre de La Orden griega. Se erige como guardiana del pergamino, y de sus secretos, y ahora Marta está no sólo al descubierto, sino aparentemente involucrada en la supuesta misión que la secta cree estar destinada a desempeñar.\n\nPero Marta no estará sola. Atreverse a no contar con ayuda supondría un resultado de la historia nada alentador para ella. Afortunadamente su mejor amigo, Alexandros, estará a su lado en el camino de la trama. Juntos seguirán las pistas que los grandes maestres de la secta, a lo largo del tiempo y de la Historia, dejaron atrás. Sólo hay que saber buscar y, sobre todo, interpretar. Ahí radica su éxito, y ahí radica su propia seguridad. Puesto que el gran secreto que se esconde tras esta aventura es conocer a no sólo a una especie legendaria, sino conocer en cierta medida el camino que la propia humanidad ha seguido en su desarrollo.\n\nEl protocolo griego es un thriller que entremezcla misterio, historia, aventura y, según el momento y su contexto, un cierto halo de terror light que encaja a la perfección dentro de la trama. La cuidada puesta en escena de los personajes, y los cuidados escenarios que el autor, Kendall Maison, ha elegido para el desarrollo de la obra, hacen que el lector se vea inmerso en una historia por países tan dispares como Turquía, Egipto o España, y por tramos de la propia Historia del hombre que le dan a El protocolo griego todos los ingredientes para ser una novela de éxito.<\/span>\n  <a       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/8835.Tim_Powers">About Tim Powers</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/8835.Tim_Powers"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1373471978p3/8835.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/8835.Tim_Powers">Tim Powers
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
1,431
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer6747091976500299338"><b>Timothy Thomas Powers</b> is an American science fiction and fantasy author. Powers has won the World Fantasy Award twice for his critically acclaimed novels Last Call and Declare.<br/><br/>Most of Powers's novels are <i>"secret histories"</i>: he uses actual, documented historical events featuring famous people, but shows another view of them in which occult or supernatural factors heavily influence the motivations a</span>
  <span id="freeText6747091976500299338" style="display:none"><b>Timothy Thomas Powers</b> is an American science fiction and fantasy author. Powers has won the World Fantasy Award twice for his critically acclaimed novels Last Call and Declare.<br /><br />Most of Powers's novels are <i>"secret histories"</i>: he uses actual, documented historical events featuring famous people, but shows another view of them in which occult or supernatural factors heavily influence the motivations and actions of the characters.<br /><br /><br />Powers was born in Buffalo, New York, and grew up in California, where his Roman Catholic family moved in 1959.<br /><br />He studied English Literature at Cal State Fullerton, where he first met <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/86475.James_Blaylock" title="James Blaylock" rel="nofollow noopener">James Blaylock</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1003655.K_W__Jeter" title="K.W. Jeter" rel="nofollow noopener">K.W. Jeter</a>, both of whom remained close friends and occasional collaborators; the trio have half-seriously referred to themselves as "steampunks" in contrast to the prevailing cyberpunk genre of the 1980s. Powers and Blaylock invented the poet <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/205455.William_Ashbless" title="William Ashbless" rel="nofollow noopener">William Ashbless</a> while they were at Cal State Fullerton.<br /><br />Another friend Powers first met during this period was noted science fiction writer <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4764.Philip_K__Dick" title="Philip K. Dick" rel="nofollow noopener">Philip K. Dick</a>; the character named "David" in Dick's novel <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/216377.VALIS" title="VALIS" rel="nofollow noopener">VALIS</a> is based on Powers and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7082.Do_Androids_Dream_of_Electric_Sheep_" title="Do Androids Dream of Electric Sheep?" rel="nofollow noopener">Do Androids Dream of Electric Sheep?</a> (Blade Runner) is dedicated to him.<br /><br />Powers's first major novel was <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/209693.The_Drawing_of_the_Dark" title="The Drawing of the Dark" rel="nofollow noopener">The Drawing of the Dark</a> (1979), but the novel that earned him wide praise was <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/142296.The_Anubis_Gates" title="The Anubis Gates" rel="nofollow noopener">The Anubis Gates</a>, which won the Philip K. Dick Award, and has since been published in many other languages.<br /><br />Powers also teaches part-time in his role as Writer in Residence for the Orange County High School of the Arts where his friend, Blaylock, is Director of the Creative Writing Department. Powers and his wife, Serena, currently live in Muscoy, California. He has frequently served as a mentor author as part of the Clarion science fiction/fantasy writer's workshop.<br /><br />He also taught part time at the University of Redlands.<br /><br /><i>Excerpted from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tim_Powers" rel="nofollow noopener">Wikipedia</a>.</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="6747091976500299338" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/2193115-the-anubis-gates">Quotes from The Anubis Gates</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Thus Milton refines the question down to a matter of faith," said Coleridge, bringing the lecture to a close, "and a kind of faith more independent, autonomous - more truly strong, as a matter of fact - than the Puritans really sought. Faith, he tells us, is not an exotic bloom to be laboriously maintained by the exclusion of most aspects of the day to day world, nor a useful delusion to be supported by sophistries and half-truths like a child's belief in Father Christmas - not, in short, a prudently unregarded adherence to a constructed creed; but rather must be, if anything, a clear-eyed <i>recognition</i> of the patterns and tendencies, to be found in every piece of the world's fabric, which are the lineaments of God. This is why religion can only be advice and clarification, and cannot carry any spurs of enforcement - for only belief and behavior that is independently arrived at, and then chosen, can be praised or blamed. This being the case, it can be seen as a criminal abridgement of a person's rights willfully to keep him in ignorance of <i>any</i> facts - no piece can be judged inadmissible, for the more stones, both bright and dark, that are added to the mosaic, the clearer is our picture of God.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/343610-thus-milton-refines-the-question-down-to-a-matter-of">14 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;The Spoonsize Boys steal the dollhouse toys while the cat by the fire is curled. Then away they floats in their eggshell boats, down the drains to their underground world.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/653193-the-spoonsize-boys-steal-the-dollhouse-toys-while-the-cat">12 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
